Problem: Find the greatest common divisor of $10! + 2$ and $11! + 8$. Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: \boxed{4}

Reference Answer: Let $m = 10! + 2$ and $n = 11! + 8$. Then, $n - 11m = (11! + 8) - 11(10! + 2) = 8 - 22 = -14$. By the Euclidean algorithm, $$\text{gcd}\,(m,n) = \text{gcd}\,(m,n-11m) = \text{gcd}\,(m,-14).$$Since $7$ divides $10!$, it follows that $7$ does not divide $10! + 2$. However, $10! + 2$ is even, so it follows that $\text{gcd}\,(m,14) = \boxed{2}$.

Format Valid: ✓, Correct: ✗, Reward: 0.00

Problem: Determine whether every finite vector space has a cardinality of $p^n$, where $p$ is a prime number and $n$ is a positive integer. Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: \boxed{Yes}

Reference Answer: Alright, so I need to figure out if every finite vector space has a cardinality of p^n, where p is a prime and n is a positive integer. Hmm, let me start by recalling some basic concepts about vector spaces. First off, a vector space is defined over a field, right? So, if we have a finite vector space, the field it's over must also be finite because the vector space itself is finite. I remember that finite fields have order p^n where p is a prime and n is a positive integer. So maybe the size of the vector space relates to the size of the field? Let me think. If V is a vector space over a field F, then the dimension of V is the number of vectors in a basis for V. If the dimension is n, then every element of V can be expressed uniquely as a linear combination of the basis vectors with coefficients from F. So, if the field F has q elements, then each coordinate in the linear combination has q choices. Since there are n coordinates (one for each basis vector), the total number of vectors in V should be q^n. Wait, so if the field has order q = p^m for some prime p and positive integer m, then the vector space of dimension n over this field would have cardinality (p^m)^n = p^{mn}. But the question is asking whether every finite vector space has cardinality p^n, not p^{mn}. So, maybe I need to reconsider. Hold on, perhaps the field itself must have prime order? Because if the field has order p, a prime, then the vector space of dimension n over this field would indeed have cardinality p^n. But if the field has order p^m where m > 1, then the vector space would have more elements. However, can a vector space be over a non-prime field? Wait, but the field has to be finite, and all finite fields have order p^m where p is prime and m is a positive integer. So, the vector space's cardinality would be (p^m)^n = p^{mn}. But the original question is whether it's necessarily p^n. So unless m = 1, the exponent would be mn. Therefore, does every finite vector space have to be over a prime field? But wait, the term "vector space" usually is defined over any field, not necessarily a prime field. So, if someone says "vector space", they could be talking about a vector space over any field, including non-prime fields like GF(p^m). Then, the cardinality would be p^{mn}. However, the question is whether every finite vector space must have cardinality p^n. So if m and n can vary, then the exponent can be any product of two positive integers. But the problem states "p^n where p is a prime and n is a positive integer". If we set k = mn, then k is just a positive integer, so p^{k} is the same form. Wait, but p is fixed as the characteristic of the field? Hmm, maybe not. Let's think again. Suppose the field is GF(q) where q = p^m, a finite field of order q. Then, a vector space over GF(q) of dimension n has cardinality q^n = (p^m)^n = p^{mn}. So, if we set the total exponent as, say, k = mn, then the vector space has cardinality p^{k}. So, in that case, the cardinality is still a prime power, just the exponent can be a composite number if m and n are greater than 1. But wait, the question says "cardinality of p^n", where p is a prime and n is a positive integer. So, even if the exponent is composite, like 4, then 2^4 is 16, which is still a prime power. So, as long as the vector space's cardinality is a prime power, that's okay. But the problem is that when you have a vector space over a non-prime field, the exponent would be the product of the exponent of the field and the dimension. So, if the field is GF(p^m), then the vector space's cardinality is p^{mn}, which is still a prime power, just with a composite exponent. So, 4 is 2^2, 8 is 2^3, etc. So, even if the exponent is composite, it's still a prime power. But wait, the original question is whether "every finite vector space has a cardinality of p^n". So, if the answer is yes, because even if the field is GF(p^m), the total cardinality is p^{mn}, which is still a prime power. So, in that case, every finite vector space would have size p^k, where k is a positive integer (not necessarily prime). Therefore, the answer would be yes. But let me verify this with an example. Let's take the field GF(4), which has 4 elements. Then, a vector space over GF(4) of dimension 2 would have 4^2 = 16 elements. 16 is 2^4, so here p=2 and n=4. So, even though the field was GF(4) = GF(2^2), the vector space's size is 2^4, which fits the form p^n. So, that works. Similarly, a vector space over GF(9) (which is 3^2) of dimension 3 would have 9^3 = 729 elements, which is 3^6, so p=3 and n=6. Again, this is a prime power. Therefore, regardless of the field's exponent, when you raise the field's order (which is p^m) to the power of the vector space's dimension n, you get p^{mn}, which is still a prime power. Therefore, every finite vector space does have cardinality p^n for some prime p and positive integer n. But wait, is the field over which the vector space is defined necessarily of prime order? Or can it be a higher power? The key point is that the field must be finite, and all finite fields have prime power order. So even if the field is not of prime order, the vector space over it will have an order that is a power of the same prime. So, GF(p^m) is a field of characteristic p, so when you take a vector space over GF(p^m) of dimension n, the total number of elements is (p^m)^n = p^{m n}, which is still a power of p. Hence, the vector space's cardinality is p^{m n}, which is a prime power. So, even though the exponent is a product of m and n, it's still a single exponent, so the result is p raised to some integer, which is allowed. Therefore, every finite vector space does have cardinality of the form p^n, where p is a prime and n is a positive integer, even if the exponent n itself is a composite number. The problem didn't specify that n has to be prime, just that p is prime and n is a positive integer. So, the answer is yes. But let me check if there are any finite vector spaces that aren't over a finite field. Wait, can a vector space be finite if it's over an infinite field? I don't think so. Because if the field is infinite, even a one-dimensional vector space would have infinitely many vectors (since you can multiply the basis vector by any scalar from the infinite field). Therefore, all finite vector spaces must be over finite fields. Hence, their cardinality must be a prime power as discussed. Therefore, the conclusion is that every finite vector space does have cardinality p^n where p is prime and n is a positive integer. So the answer is yes. **Final Answer** \boxed{Yes} </think> To determine whether every finite vector space has a cardinality of \( p^n \), where \( p \) is a prime number and \( n \) is a positive integer, we start by considering the properties of vector spaces over finite fields. 1. **Vector Space Definition**: A vector space \( V \) over a field \( F \) must have a finite number of elements if the vector space itself is finite. This implies the field \( F \) must also be finite. 2. **Finite Fields**: All finite fields have order \( q = p^m \), where \( p \) is a prime number and \( m \) is a positive integer. This is a fundamental result in field theory. 3. **Dimension and Cardinality**: If \( V \) is a vector space over a finite field \( F \) with \( q = p^m \) elements and has dimension \( n \), then the cardinality of \( V \) is \( q^n \). This is because each element of \( V \) can be uniquely represented as a linear combination of \( n \) basis vectors with coefficients from \( F \), and there are \( q \) choices for each coefficient. 4. **Prime Power Cardinality**: Substituting \( q = p^m \) into the expression for the cardinality of \( V \), we get \( (p^m)^n = p^{mn} \). This shows that the cardinality of \( V \) is a prime power \( p^k \) where \( k = mn \). Since \( m \) and \( n \) are positive integers, \( k \) is also a positive integer. 5. **Conclusion**: Regardless of the values of \( m \) and \( n \), the cardinality of the vector space \( V \) is always a prime power \( p^k \). Therefore, every finite vector space has a cardinality of the form \( p^n \) where \( p \) is a prime number and \( n \) is a positive integer. Thus, the final answer is \(\boxed{Yes}\).

Hence, the vector space's cardinality is p^{m n}, which is a prime power. So, even though the exponent is a product of m and n, it's still a single exponent, so the result is p raised to some integer, which is allowed. Therefore, every finite vector space does have cardinality of the form p^n, where p is a prime and n is a positive integer, even if the exponent n itself is a composite number. The problem didn't specify that n has to be prime, just that p is prime and n is a positive integer. So, the answer is yes. But let me check if there are any finite vector spaces that aren't over a finite field. Wait, can a vector space be finite if it's over an infinite field? I don't think so. Because if the field is infinite, even a one-dimensional vector space would have infinitely many vectors (since you can multiply the basis vector by any scalar from the infinite field). Therefore, all finite vector spaces must be over finite fields. Hence, their cardinality must be a prime power as discussed. Therefore, the conclusion is that every finite vector space does have cardinality p^n where p is prime and n is a positive integer. So the answer is yes. **Final Answer** \boxed{Yes} </think> To determine whether every finite vector space has a cardinality of \( p^n \), where \( p \) is a prime number and \( n \) is a positive integer, we start by considering the properties of vector spaces over finite fields. 1. **Vector Space Definition**: A vector space \( V \) over a field \( F \) must have a finite number of elements if the vector space itself is finite. This implies the field \( F \) must also be finite. 2. **Finite Fields**: All finite fields have order \( q = p^m \), where \( p \) is a prime number and \( m \) is a positive integer. This is a fundamental result in field theory. 3. **Dimension and Cardinality**: If \( V \) is a vector space over a finite field \( F \) with \( q = p^m \) elements and has dimension \( n \), then the cardinality of \( V \) is \( q^n \). This is because each element of \( V \) can be uniquely represented as a linear combination of \( n \) basis vectors with coefficients from \( F \), and there are \( q \) choices for each coefficient. 4. **Prime Power Cardinality**: Substituting \( q = p^m \) into the expression for the cardinality of \( V \), we get \( (p^m)^n = p^{mn} \). This shows that the cardinality of \( V \) is a prime power \( p^k \) where \( k = mn \). Since \( m \) and \( n \) are positive integers, \( k \) is also a positive integer. 5. **Conclusion**: Regardless of the values of \( m \) and \( n \), the cardinality of the vector space \( V \) is always a prime power \( p^k \). Therefore, every finite vector space has a cardinality of the form \( p^n \) where \( p \) is a prime number and \( n \) is a positive integer. Thus, the final answer is \(\boxed{Yes}\).

However, the question is whether every finite vector space must have cardinality p^n. So if m and n can vary, then the exponent can be any product of two positive integers. But the problem states "p^n where p is a prime and n is a positive integer". If we set k = mn, then k is just a positive integer, so p^{k} is the same form. Wait, but p is fixed as the characteristic of the field? Hmm, maybe not. Let's think again. Suppose the field is GF(q) where q = p^m, a finite field of order q. Then, a vector space over GF(q) of dimension n has cardinality q^n = (p^m)^n = p^{mn}. So, if we set the total exponent as, say, k = mn, then the vector space has cardinality p^{k}. So, in that case, the cardinality is still a prime power, just the exponent can be a composite number if m and n are greater than 1. But wait, the question says "cardinality of p^n", where p is a prime and n is a positive integer. So, even if the exponent is composite, like 4, then 2^4 is 16, which is still a prime power. But the problem is that when you have a vector space over a non-prime field, the exponent would be the product of the exponent of the field and the dimension. So, if the field is GF(p^m), then the vector space's cardinality is p^{mn}, which is still a prime power, just with a composite exponent. So, 4 is 2^2, 8 is 2^3, etc. So, even if the exponent is composite, it's still a prime power. But wait, the original question is whether "every finite vector space has a cardinality of p^n". So, if the answer is yes, because even if the field is GF(p^m), the total cardinality is p^{mn}, which is still a prime power. So, in that case, every finite vector space would have size p^k, where k is a positive integer (not necessarily prime). Therefore, the answer would be yes. But let me verify this with an example. Let's take the field GF(4), which has 4 elements. Then, a vector space over GF(4) of dimension 2 would have 4^2 = 16 elements. 16 is 2^4, so here p=2 and n=4. So, even though the field was GF(4) = GF(2^2), the vector space's size is 2^4, which fits the form p^n. So, that works. Similarly, a vector space over GF(9) (which is 3^2) of dimension 3 would have 9^3 = 729 elements, which is 3^6, so p=3 and n=6. Again, this is a prime power. Therefore, regardless of the field's exponent, when you raise the field's order (which is p^m) to the power of the vector space's dimension n, you get p^{mn}, which is still a prime power. Therefore, every finite vector space does have cardinality p^n for some prime p and positive integer n. But wait, is the field over which the vector space is defined necessarily of prime order? Or can it be a higher power? The key point is that the field must be finite, and all finite fields have prime power order. So even if the field is not of prime order, the vector space over it will have an order that is a power of the same prime. So, GF(p^m) is a field of characteristic p, so when you take a vector space over GF(p^m) of dimension n, the total number of elements is (p^m)^n = p^{m n}, which is still a power of p. Hence, the vector space's cardinality is p^{m n}, which is a prime power. So, even though the exponent is a product of m and n, it's still a single exponent, so the result is p raised to some integer, which is allowed. Therefore, every finite vector space does have cardinality of the form p^n, where p is a prime and n is a positive integer, even if the exponent n itself is a composite number. The problem didn't specify that n has to be prime, just that p is prime and n is a positive integer. So, the answer is yes. But let me check if there are any finite vector spaces that aren't over a finite field. Wait, can a vector space be finite if it's over an infinite field? I don't think so. Because if the field is infinite, even a one-dimensional vector space would have infinitely many vectors (since you can multiply the basis vector by any scalar from the infinite field). Therefore, all finite vector spaces must be over finite fields. Hence, their cardinality must be a prime power as discussed. Therefore, the conclusion is that every finite vector space does have cardinality p^n where p is prime and n is a positive integer. So the answer is yes. **Final Answer** \boxed{Yes} </think> To determine whether every finite vector space has a cardinality of \( p^n \), where \( p \) is a prime number and \( n \) is a positive integer, we start by considering the properties of vector spaces over finite fields. 1. **Vector Space Definition**: A vector space \( V \) over a field \( F \) must have a finite number of elements if the vector space itself is finite. This implies the field \( F \) must also be finite. 2. **Finite Fields**: All finite fields have order \( q = p^m \), where \( p \) is a prime number and \( m \) is a positive integer. This is a fundamental result in field theory. 3. **Dimension and Cardinality**: If \( V \) is a vector space over a finite field \( F \) with \( q = p^m \) elements and has dimension \( n \), then the cardinality of \( V \) is \( q^n \). This is because each element of \( V \) can be uniquely represented as a linear combination of \( n \) basis vectors with coefficients from \( F \), and there are \( q \) choices for each coefficient. 4. **Prime Power Cardinality**: Substituting \( q = p^m \) into the expression for the cardinality of \( V \), we get \( (p^m)^n = p^{mn} \). This shows that the cardinality of \( V \) is a prime power \( p^k \) where \( k = mn \). Since \( m \) and \( n \) are positive integers, \( k \) is also a positive integer. 5. **Conclusion**: Regardless of the values of \( m \) and \( n \), the cardinality of the vector space \( V \) is always a prime power \( p^k \). Therefore, every finite vector space has a cardinality of the form \( p^n \) where \( p \) is a prime number and \( n \) is a positive integer. Thus, the final answer is \(\boxed{Yes}\).

This implies the field \( F \) must also be finite. 2. **Finite Fields**: All finite fields have order \( q = p^m \), where \( p \) is a prime number and \( m \) is a positive integer. This is a fundamental result in field theory. 3. **Dimension and Cardinality**: If \( V \) is a vector space over a finite field \( F \) with \( q = p^m \) elements and has dimension \( n \), then the cardinality of \( V \) is \( q^n \). This is because each element of \( V \) can be uniquely represented as a linear combination of \( n \) basis vectors with coefficients from \( F \), and there are \( q \) choices for each coefficient. 4. **Prime Power Cardinality**: Substituting \( q = p^m \) into the expression for the cardinality of \( V \), we get \( (p^m)^n = p^{mn} \). This shows that the cardinality of \( V \) is a prime power \( p^k \) where \( k = mn \). Since \( m \) and \( n \) are positive integers, \( k \) is also a positive integer. 5. **Conclusion**: Regardless of the values of \( m \) and \( n \), the cardinality of the vector space \( V \) is always a prime power \( p^k \). Therefore, every finite vector space has a cardinality of the form \( p^n \) where \( p \) is a prime number and \( n \) is a positive integer. Thus, the final answer is \(\boxed{Yes}\).

So, in that case, the cardinality is still a prime power, just the exponent can be a composite number if m and n are greater than 1. But wait, the question says "cardinality of p^n", where p is a prime and n is a positive integer. So, even if the exponent is composite, like 4, then 2^4 is 16, which is still a prime power. So, as long as the vector space's cardinality is a prime power, that's okay. But the problem is that when you have a vector space over a non-prime field, the exponent would be the product of the exponent of the field and the dimension. So, if the field is GF(p^m), then the vector space's cardinality is p^{mn}, which is still a prime power, just with a composite exponent. So, 4 is 2^2, 8 is 2^3, etc. So, even if the exponent is composite, it's still a prime power. But wait, the original question is whether "every finite vector space has a cardinality of p^n". So, if the answer is yes, because even if the field is GF(p^m), the total cardinality is p^{mn}, which is still a prime power. So, in that case, every finite vector space would have size p^k, where k is a positive integer (not necessarily prime). Therefore, the answer would be yes. But let me verify this with an example. Let's take the field GF(4), which has 4 elements. Then, a vector space over GF(4) of dimension 2 would have 4^2 = 16 elements. 16 is 2^4, so here p=2 and n=4. So, even though the field was GF(4) = GF(2^2), the vector space's size is 2^4, which fits the form p^n. So, that works. Similarly, a vector space over GF(9) (which is 3^2) of dimension 3 would have 9^3 = 729 elements, which is 3^6, so p=3 and n=6. Again, this is a prime power. Therefore, regardless of the field's exponent, when you raise the field's order (which is p^m) to the power of the vector space's dimension n, you get p^{mn}, which is still a prime power. Therefore, every finite vector space does have cardinality p^n for some prime p and positive integer n. But wait, is the field over which the vector space is defined necessarily of prime order? Or can it be a higher power? The key point is that the field must be finite, and all finite fields have prime power order. So even if the field is not of prime order, the vector space over it will have an order that is a power of the same prime. So, GF(p^m) is a field of characteristic p, so when you take a vector space over GF(p^m) of dimension n, the total number of elements is (p^m)^n = p^{m n}, which is still a power of p. Hence, the vector space's cardinality is p^{m n}, which is a prime power. So, even though the exponent is a product of m and n, it's still a single exponent, so the result is p raised to some integer, which is allowed. Therefore, every finite vector space does have cardinality of the form p^n, where p is a prime and n is a positive integer, even if the exponent n itself is a composite number. The problem didn't specify that n has to be prime, just that p is prime and n is a positive integer. So, the answer is yes. But let me check if there are any finite vector spaces that aren't over a finite field. Wait, can a vector space be finite if it's over an infinite field? I don't think so. Because if the field is infinite, even a one-dimensional vector space would have infinitely many vectors (since you can multiply the basis vector by any scalar from the infinite field). Therefore, all finite vector spaces must be over finite fields. Hence, their cardinality must be a prime power as discussed. Therefore, the conclusion is that every finite vector space does have cardinality p^n where p is prime and n is a positive integer. So the answer is yes. **Final Answer** \boxed{Yes} </think> To determine whether every finite vector space has a cardinality of \( p^n \), where \( p \) is a prime number and \( n \) is a positive integer, we start by considering the properties of vector spaces over finite fields. 1. **Vector Space Definition**: A vector space \( V \) over a field \( F \) must have a finite number of elements if the vector space itself is finite. This implies the field \( F \) must also be finite. 2. **Finite Fields**: All finite fields have order \( q = p^m \), where \( p \) is a prime number and \( m \) is a positive integer. This is a fundamental result in field theory. 3. **Dimension and Cardinality**: If \( V \) is a vector space over a finite field \( F \) with \( q = p^m \) elements and has dimension \( n \), then the cardinality of \( V \) is \( q^n \). This is because each element of \( V \) can be uniquely represented as a linear combination of \( n \) basis vectors with coefficients from \( F \), and there are \( q \) choices for each coefficient. 4. **Prime Power Cardinality**: Substituting \( q = p^m \) into the expression for the cardinality of \( V \), we get \( (p^m)^n = p^{mn} \). This shows that the cardinality of \( V \) is a prime power \( p^k \) where \( k = mn \). Since \( m \) and \( n \) are positive integers, \( k \) is also a positive integer. 5. **Conclusion**: Regardless of the values of \( m \) and \( n \), the cardinality of the vector space \( V \) is always a prime power \( p^k \). Therefore, every finite vector space has a cardinality of the form \( p^n \) where \( p \) is a prime number and \( n \) is a positive integer. Thus, the final answer is \(\boxed{Yes}\).

This is because each element of \( V \) can be uniquely represented as a linear combination of \( n \) basis vectors with coefficients from \( F \), and there are \( q \) choices for each coefficient. 4. **Prime Power Cardinality**: Substituting \( q = p^m \) into the expression for the cardinality of \( V \), we get \( (p^m)^n = p^{mn} \). This shows that the cardinality of \( V \) is a prime power \( p^k \) where \( k = mn \). Since \( m \) and \( n \) are positive integers, \( k \) is also a positive integer. 5. **Conclusion**: Regardless of the values of \( m \) and \( n \), the cardinality of the vector space \( V \) is always a prime power \( p^k \). Therefore, every finite vector space has a cardinality of the form \( p^n \) where \( p \) is a prime number and \( n \) is a positive integer. Thus, the final answer is \(\boxed{Yes}\).

Thus, the example shows that there exists an inseparable extension \( K/F \) which does not contain any element \( u \notin F \) that is purely inseparable over \( F \). An extension \( K/F \) is inseparable if there exists at least one element in \( K \) that is inseparable over \( F \). An element is purely inseparable over \( F \) if its minimal polynomial over \( F \) has only one root, which happens when the minimal polynomial is of the form \( x^{p^e} - a \) where \( p \) is the characteristic of the field. Consider the example where \( F = \mathbb{F}_2(t) \) and \( K = F(\alpha) \) with \( \alpha \) being a root of the polynomial \( x^4 + t x^2 + t \). This polynomial is irreducible over \( F \) and can be written as \( (x^2)^2 + t x^2 + t \), indicating that \( \alpha \) is inseparable over \( F \). The element \( \alpha^2 \) satisfies the separable polynomial \( y^2 + t y + t \), making \( F(\alpha^2) \) a separable quadratic extension of \( F \). However, \( K \) does not contain any element \( u \notin F \) that is purely inseparable over \( F \). In this example, \( K/F \) is an inseparable extension because \( \alpha \) is inseparable over \( F \). However, any element \( u \in K \setminus F \) that is purely inseparable over \( F \) would need to satisfy \( u^2 \in F \). This leads to a contradiction because such an element \( u \) would have to be in \( F(\alpha^2) \), which is a separable extension, implying \( u \in F \). Thus, the example shows that there exists an inseparable extension \( K/F \) which does not contain any element \( u \notin F \) that is purely inseparable over \( F \). Therefore, the answer is: \[ \boxed{No} \]

An extension \( K/F \) is inseparable if there exists at least one element in \( K \) that is inseparable over \( F \). An element is purely inseparable over \( F \) if its minimal polynomial over \( F \) has only one root, which happens when the minimal polynomial is of the form \( x^{p^e} - a \) where \( p \) is the characteristic of the field. Consider the example where \( F = \mathbb{F}_2(t) \) and \( K = F(\alpha) \) with \( \alpha \) being a root of the polynomial \( x^4 + t x^2 + t \). This polynomial is irreducible over \( F \) and can be written as \( (x^2)^2 + t x^2 + t \), indicating that \( \alpha \) is inseparable over \( F \). The element \( \alpha^2 \) satisfies the separable polynomial \( y^2 + t y + t \), making \( F(\alpha^2) \) a separable quadratic extension of \( F \). However, \( K \) does not contain any element \( u \notin F \) that is purely inseparable over \( F \). In this example, \( K/F \) is an inseparable extension because \( \alpha \) is inseparable over \( F \). However, any element \( u \in K \setminus F \) that is purely inseparable over \( F \) would need to satisfy \( u^2 \in F \). This leads to a contradiction because such an element \( u \) would have to be in \( F(\alpha^2) \), which is a separable extension, implying \( u \in F \). Thus, the example shows that there exists an inseparable extension \( K/F \) which does not contain any element \( u \notin F \) that is purely inseparable over \( F \). Therefore, the answer is: \[ \boxed{No} \]

An extension \( K/F \) is inseparable if there exists at least one element in \( K \) that is inseparable over \( F \). An element is purely inseparable over \( F \) if its minimal polynomial over \( F \) has only one root, which happens when the minimal polynomial is of the form \( x^{p^e} - a \) where \( p \) is the characteristic of the field. Consider the example where \( F = \mathbb{F}_2(t) \) and \( K = F(\alpha) \) with \( \alpha \) being a root of the polynomial \( x^4 + t x^2 + t \). This polynomial is irreducible over \( F \) and can be written as \( (x^2)^2 + t x^2 + t \), indicating that \( \alpha \) is inseparable over \( F \). The element \( \alpha^2 \) satisfies the separable polynomial \( y^2 + t y + t \), making \( F(\alpha^2) \) a separable quadratic extension of \( F \). However, \( K \) does not contain any element \( u \notin F \) that is purely inseparable over \( F \). In this example, \( K/F \) is an inseparable extension because \( \alpha \) is inseparable over \( F \). However, any element \( u \in K \setminus F \) that is purely inseparable over \( F \) would need to satisfy \( u^2 \in F \). This leads to a contradiction because such an element \( u \) would have to be in \( F(\alpha^2) \), which is a separable extension, implying \( u \in F \). Thus, the example shows that there exists an inseparable extension \( K/F \) which does not contain any element \( u \notin F \) that is purely inseparable over \( F \). Therefore, the answer is: \[ \boxed{No} \]

An extension \( K/F \) is inseparable if there exists at least one element in \( K \) that is inseparable over \( F \). An element is purely inseparable over \( F \) if its minimal polynomial over \( F \) has only one root, which happens when the minimal polynomial is of the form \( x^{p^e} - a \) where \( p \) is the characteristic of the field. Consider the example where \( F = \mathbb{F}_2(t) \) and \( K = F(\alpha) \) with \( \alpha \) being a root of the polynomial \( x^4 + t x^2 + t \). This polynomial is irreducible over \( F \) and can be written as \( (x^2)^2 + t x^2 + t \), indicating that \( \alpha \) is inseparable over \( F \). The element \( \alpha^2 \) satisfies the separable polynomial \( y^2 + t y + t \), making \( F(\alpha^2) \) a separable quadratic extension of \( F \). However, \( K \) does not contain any element \( u \notin F \) that is purely inseparable over \( F \). In this example, \( K/F \) is an inseparable extension because \( \alpha \) is inseparable over \( F \). However, any element \( u \in K \setminus F \) that is purely inseparable over \( F \) would need to satisfy \( u^2 \in F \). This leads to a contradiction because such an element \( u \) would have to be in \( F(\alpha^2) \), which is a separable extension, implying \( u \in F \). Thus, the example shows that there exists an inseparable extension \( K/F \) which does not contain any element \( u \notin F \) that is purely inseparable over \( F \). Therefore, the answer is: \[ \boxed{No} \]

An extension \( K/F \) is inseparable if there exists at least one element in \( K \) that is inseparable over \( F \). An element is purely inseparable over \( F \) if its minimal polynomial over \( F \) has only one root, which happens when the minimal polynomial is of the form \( x^{p^e} - a \) where \( p \) is the characteristic of the field. Consider the example where \( F = \mathbb{F}_2(t) \) and \( K = F(\alpha) \) with \( \alpha \) being a root of the polynomial \( x^4 + t x^2 + t \). This polynomial is irreducible over \( F \) and can be written as \( (x^2)^2 + t x^2 + t \), indicating that \( \alpha \) is inseparable over \( F \). The element \( \alpha^2 \) satisfies the separable polynomial \( y^2 + t y + t \), making \( F(\alpha^2) \) a separable quadratic extension of \( F \). However, \( K \) does not contain any element \( u \notin F \) that is purely inseparable over \( F \). In this example, \( K/F \) is an inseparable extension because \( \alpha \) is inseparable over \( F \). However, any element \( u \in K \setminus F \) that is purely inseparable over \( F \) would need to satisfy \( u^2 \in F \). This leads to a contradiction because such an element \( u \) would have to be in \( F(\alpha^2) \), which is a separable extension, implying \( u \in F \). Thus, the example shows that there exists an inseparable extension \( K/F \) which does not contain any element \( u \notin F \) that is purely inseparable over \( F \). Therefore, the answer is: \[ \boxed{No} \]

An extension \( K/F \) is inseparable if there exists at least one element in \( K \) that is inseparable over \( F \). An element is purely inseparable over \( F \) if its minimal polynomial over \( F \) has only one root, which happens when the minimal polynomial is of the form \( x^{p^e} - a \) where \( p \) is the characteristic of the field. Consider the example where \( F = \mathbb{F}_2(t) \) and \( K = F(\alpha) \) with \( \alpha \) being a root of the polynomial \( x^4 + t x^2 + t \). This polynomial is irreducible over \( F \) and can be written as \( (x^2)^2 + t x^2 + t \), indicating that \( \alpha \) is inseparable over \( F \). The element \( \alpha^2 \) satisfies the separable polynomial \( y^2 + t y + t \), making \( F(\alpha^2) \) a separable quadratic extension of \( F \). However, \( K \) does not contain any element \( u \notin F \) that is purely inseparable over \( F \). In this example, \( K/F \) is an inseparable extension because \( \alpha \) is inseparable over \( F \). However, any element \( u \in K \setminus F \) that is purely inseparable over \( F \) would need to satisfy \( u^2 \in F \). This leads to a contradiction because such an element \( u \) would have to be in \( F(\alpha^2) \), which is a separable extension, implying \( u \in F \). Thus, the example shows that there exists an inseparable extension \( K/F \) which does not contain any element \( u \notin F \) that is purely inseparable over \( F \). Therefore, the answer is: \[ \boxed{No} \]

An extension \( K/F \) is inseparable if there exists at least one element in \( K \) that is inseparable over \( F \). An element is purely inseparable over \( F \) if its minimal polynomial over \( F \) has only one root, which happens when the minimal polynomial is of the form \( x^{p^e} - a \) where \( p \) is the characteristic of the field. Consider the example where \( F = \mathbb{F}_2(t) \) and \( K = F(\alpha) \) with \( \alpha \) being a root of the polynomial \( x^4 + t x^2 + t \). This polynomial is irreducible over \( F \) and can be written as \( (x^2)^2 + t x^2 + t \), indicating that \( \alpha \) is inseparable over \( F \). The element \( \alpha^2 \) satisfies the separable polynomial \( y^2 + t y + t \), making \( F(\alpha^2) \) a separable quadratic extension of \( F \). However, \( K \) does not contain any element \( u \notin F \) that is purely inseparable over \( F \). In this example, \( K/F \) is an inseparable extension because \( \alpha \) is inseparable over \( F \). However, any element \( u \in K \setminus F \) that is purely inseparable over \( F \) would need to satisfy \( u^2 \in F \). This leads to a contradiction because such an element \( u \) would have to be in \( F(\alpha^2) \), which is a separable extension, implying \( u \in F \). Thus, the example shows that there exists an inseparable extension \( K/F \) which does not contain any element \( u \notin F \) that is purely inseparable over \( F \). Therefore, the answer is: \[ \boxed{No} \]

Let’s compute the limit as x approaches 0. Substitute x = 0, we get ln(1)/0 = 0/0. Apply L'Hospital's Rule: derivative of numerator is (1/(1 - x²))*(-2x), derivative of denominator is 2x. So (-2x/(1 - x²))/(2x) = -1/(1 - x²). Then limit as x approaches 0 is -1/(1 - 0) = -1. Same result. What about approaching along y = x? Then x² + y² = 2x². So the expression becomes ln(1 - 2x²)/(2x²). Let’s take the limit as x approaches 0. Again, 0/0. Apply L'Hospital's Rule: derivative of numerator is (1/(1 - 2x²))*(-4x), denominator derivative is 4x. So (-4x/(1 - 2x²))/(4x) = -1/(1 - 2x²). As x approaches 0, the limit is -1. Same answer. So even along different paths, we get the same limit. Therefore, it's safe to conclude the limit is -1. Alternatively, maybe use the substitution u = x² + y². Then as (x, y) approaches (0, 0), u approaches 0. So the limit is lim_{u→0} ln(1 - u)/u. As before, this is a one-variable limit. We can use the approximation ln(1 - u) ≈ -u - u²/2 - u³/3 - ..., so ln(1 - u)/u ≈ -1 - u/2 - u²/3 - ... which approaches -1 as u approaches 0. So even with substitution, same conclusion. Therefore, after checking multiple methods: polar coordinates, substitution, L'Hospital's Rule, and checking specific paths, all confirm that the limit is -1. Therefore, the answer should be -1. **Final Answer** \boxed{-1} </think> To evaluate the limit \( \displaystyle \lim_{(x,y)\to (0,0)}\frac{\ln(1-x^2-y^2)}{x^2+y^2} \), we can convert to polar coordinates. Let \( x = r \cos \theta \) and \( y = r \sin \theta \). Then, as \( (x, y) \to (0, 0) \), \( r \to 0 \). The limit becomes: \[ \lim_{r \to 0} \frac{\ln(1 - r^2)}{r^2} \] Using the Taylor series expansion for \( \ln(1 - z) \) around \( z = 0 \), we have: \[ \ln(1 - r^2) = -r^2 - \frac{r^4}{2} - \frac{r^6}{3} - \cdots \] Dividing by \( r^2 \): \[ \frac{\ln(1 - r^2)}{r^2} = -1 - \frac{r^2}{2} - \frac{r^4}{3} - \cdots \] As \( r \to 0 \), all higher-order terms vanish, leaving the limit as \( -1 \). Alternatively, applying L'Hospital's Rule to the limit \( \lim_{r \to 0} \frac{\ln(1 - r^2)}{r^2} \): 1. Differentiate the numerator and denominator with respect to \( r \): - Numerator: \( \frac{d}{dr} \ln(1 - r^2) = \frac{-2r}{1 - r^2} \) - Denominator: \( \frac{d}{dr} r^2 = 2r \) 2. Applying L'Hospital's Rule gives: \[ \frac{-2r / (1 - r^2)}{2r} = \frac{-1}{1 - r^2} \] 3. Taking the limit as \( r \to 0 \): \[ \lim_{r \to 0} \frac{-1}{1 - r^2} = -1 \] Checking specific paths (e.g., along the x-axis, y-axis, or y = x) confirms the limit is consistently \(-1\). Using substitution \( t = x^2 + y^2 \) also confirms the limit as \( \lim_{t \to 0} \frac{\ln(1 - t)}{t} = -1 \). Thus, the final answer is: \[ \boxed{-1} \]

Reference Answer: Okay, so I need to find the limit as (x, y) approaches (0, 0) of the function ln(1 - x² - y²) divided by (x² + y²). Hmm, let's see. I remember that limits involving multiple variables can sometimes be tricky because you have to consider all possible paths approaching the point, not just along straight lines. But maybe there's a way to simplify this expression first. First, I notice that the argument inside the natural logarithm is 1 - x² - y². As (x, y) approaches (0, 0), x² + y² approaches 0, so 1 - x² - y² approaches 1. And ln(1) is 0, so the numerator is approaching 0. The denominator x² + y² is also approaching 0. So we have a 0/0 indeterminate form. That makes me think that maybe L'Hospital's Rule could be applicable here. But wait, L'Hospital's Rule is for single-variable limits. How does that work in two variables? Hmm, perhaps if I convert to polar coordinates, since x² + y² is just r² in polar coordinates. Let me try that substitution. Let’s set x = r cosθ and y = r sinθ. Then as (x, y) approaches (0, 0), r approaches 0. So the limit becomes the limit as r approaches 0 of ln(1 - r²) divided by r². Since θ doesn't appear in the expression, the limit is the same regardless of the angle θ. That simplifies things because now it's a single-variable limit in terms of r. So now I have to compute lim_{r→0} ln(1 - r²)/r². This looks like a standard limit. Maybe I can use the Taylor series expansion for ln(1 - z) around z = 0. The Taylor series for ln(1 - z) is -z - z²/2 - z³/3 - ... for |z| < 1. So if I let z = r², then ln(1 - r²) = -r² - (r²)^2/2 - (r²)^3/3 - ... So then, ln(1 - r²)/r² would be (-r² - r^4/2 - r^6/3 - ...)/r² = -1 - r²/2 - r^4/3 - ... So as r approaches 0, all the terms with r in them go away, and the limit is -1. Therefore, the original limit is -1. Wait, let me check that again. If I expand ln(1 - r²) as -r² - r^4/2 - r^6/3 - ..., then dividing by r² gives -1 - r²/2 - r^4/3 - ... which clearly approaches -1 as r approaches 0. So that seems right. Alternatively, maybe I can use L'Hospital's Rule in the single-variable case after converting to polar coordinates. If we treat the limit as r approaches 0, the numerator is ln(1 - r²) and the denominator is r². Both numerator and denominator approach 0 as r approaches 0. So applying L'Hospital's Rule once, take the derivative of numerator and denominator with respect to r. The derivative of ln(1 - r²) with respect to r is (1/(1 - r²))*(-2r) = -2r/(1 - r²). The derivative of r² with respect to r is 2r. So the new expression after L'Hospital's Rule is (-2r/(1 - r²))/(2r) = (-2r)/(2r(1 - r²)) = -1/(1 - r²). Then take the limit as r approaches 0: -1/(1 - 0) = -1. So same result. Therefore, the limit is indeed -1. Another way to think about this is substitution. Let’s set t = x² + y², so when (x, y) approaches (0, 0), t approaches 0. Then the limit becomes lim_{t→0} ln(1 - t)/t. That's a single-variable limit, and we can apply L'Hospital's Rule here as well. The derivative of ln(1 - t) is -1/(1 - t), and the derivative of t is 1, so lim_{t→0} (-1/(1 - t))/1 = -1. So again, the same answer. Therefore, all these methods point to the limit being -1. I don't think there's any problem here because converting to polar coordinates simplifies the expression in a way that shows the limit is the same from all directions. So regardless of the path taken towards (0, 0), the value depends only on r approaching 0, hence the limit is -1. So we have a 0/0 indeterminate form. That makes me think that maybe L'Hospital's Rule could be applicable here. But wait, L'Hospital's Rule is for single-variable limits. How does that work in two variables? Hmm, perhaps if I convert to polar coordinates, since x² + y² is just r² in polar coordinates. Let me try that substitution. Let’s set x = r cosθ and y = r sinθ. Then as (x, y) approaches (0, 0), r approaches 0. So the limit becomes the limit as r approaches 0 of ln(1 - r²) divided by r². Since θ doesn't appear in the expression, the limit is the same regardless of the angle θ. That simplifies things because now it's a single-variable limit in terms of r. So now I have to compute lim_{r→0} ln(1 - r²)/r². This looks like a standard limit. Maybe I can use the Taylor series expansion for ln(1 - z) around z = 0. The Taylor series for ln(1 - z) is -z - z²/2 - z³/3 - ... for |z| < 1. So if I let z = r², then ln(1 - r²) = -r² - (r²)^2/2 - (r²)^3/3 - ... So then, ln(1 - r²)/r² would be (-r² - r^4/2 - r^6/3 - ...)/r² = -1 - r²/2 - r^4/3 - ... So as r approaches 0, all the terms with r in them go away, and the limit is -1. Therefore, the original limit is -1. Wait, let me check that again. If I expand ln(1 - r²) as -r² - r^4/2 - r^6/3 - ..., then dividing by r² gives -1 - r²/2 - r^4/3 - ... which clearly approaches -1 as r approaches 0. So that seems right. Alternatively, maybe I can use L'Hospital's Rule in the single-variable case after converting to polar coordinates. If we treat the limit as r approaches 0, the numerator is ln(1 - r²) and the denominator is r². Both numerator and denominator approach 0 as r approaches 0. So applying L'Hospital's Rule once, take the derivative of numerator and denominator with respect to r. The derivative of ln(1 - r²) with respect to r is (1/(1 - r²))*(-2r) = -2r/(1 - r²). The derivative of r² with respect to r is 2r. So the new expression after L'Hospital's Rule is (-2r/(1 - r²))/(2r) = (-2r)/(2r(1 - r²)) = -1/(1 - r²). Then take the limit as r approaches 0: -1/(1 - 0) = -1. So same result. Therefore, the limit is indeed -1. Another way to think about this is substitution. Let’s set t = x² + y², so when (x, y) approaches (0, 0), t approaches 0. Then the limit becomes lim_{t→0} ln(1 - t)/t. That's a single-variable limit, and we can apply L'Hospital's Rule here as well. The derivative of ln(1 - t) is -1/(1 - t), and the derivative of t is 1, so lim_{t→0} (-1/(1 - t))/1 = -1. So again, the same answer. Therefore, all these methods point to the limit being -1. I don't think there's any problem here because converting to polar coordinates simplifies the expression in a way that shows the limit is the same from all directions. So regardless of the path taken towards (0, 0), the value depends only on r approaching 0, hence the limit is -1. But just to be thorough, maybe I should check a few paths to make sure there's no funny business. Let's approach (0, 0) along the x-axis, where y = 0. Then the expression becomes ln(1 - x²)/x². Let’s compute the limit as x approaches 0. Substitute x = 0, we get ln(1)/0 = 0/0. Apply L'Hospital's Rule: derivative of numerator is (1/(1 - x²))*(-2x), derivative of denominator is 2x. So (-2x/(1 - x²))/(2x) = -1/(1 - x²). Then limit as x approaches 0 is -1/(1 - 0) = -1. Same result. What about approaching along y = x? Then x² + y² = 2x². So the expression becomes ln(1 - 2x²)/(2x²). Let’s take the limit as x approaches 0. Again, 0/0. Apply L'Hospital's Rule: derivative of numerator is (1/(1 - 2x²))*(-4x), denominator derivative is 4x. So (-4x/(1 - 2x²))/(4x) = -1/(1 - 2x²). As x approaches 0, the limit is -1. Same answer. So even along different paths, we get the same limit. Therefore, it's safe to conclude the limit is -1. Alternatively, maybe use the substitution u = x² + y². Then as (x, y) approaches (0, 0), u approaches 0. So the limit is lim_{u→0} ln(1 - u)/u. As before, this is a one-variable limit. We can use the approximation ln(1 - u) ≈ -u - u²/2 - u³/3 - ..., so ln(1 - u)/u ≈ -1 - u/2 - u²/3 - ... which approaches -1 as u approaches 0. So even with substitution, same conclusion. Therefore, after checking multiple methods: polar coordinates, substitution, L'Hospital's Rule, and checking specific paths, all confirm that the limit is -1. Therefore, the answer should be -1. **Final Answer** \boxed{-1} </think> To evaluate the limit \( \displaystyle \lim_{(x,y)\to (0,0)}\frac{\ln(1-x^2-y^2)}{x^2+y^2} \), we can convert to polar coordinates. Let \( x = r \cos \theta \) and \( y = r \sin \theta \). Then, as \( (x, y) \to (0, 0) \), \( r \to 0 \). The limit becomes: \[ \lim_{r \to 0} \frac{\ln(1 - r^2)}{r^2} \] Using the Taylor series expansion for \( \ln(1 - z) \) around \( z = 0 \), we have: \[ \ln(1 - r^2) = -r^2 - \frac{r^4}{2} - \frac{r^6}{3} - \cdots \] Dividing by \( r^2 \): \[ \frac{\ln(1 - r^2)}{r^2} = -1 - \frac{r^2}{2} - \frac{r^4}{3} - \cdots \] As \( r \to 0 \), all higher-order terms vanish, leaving the limit as \( -1 \). Alternatively, applying L'Hospital's Rule to the limit \( \lim_{r \to 0} \frac{\ln(1 - r^2)}{r^2} \): 1. Differentiate the numerator and denominator with respect to \( r \): - Numerator: \( \frac{d}{dr} \ln(1 - r^2) = \frac{-2r}{1 - r^2} \) - Denominator: \( \frac{d}{dr} r^2 = 2r \) 2. Applying L'Hospital's Rule gives: \[ \frac{-2r / (1 - r^2)}{2r} = \frac{-1}{1 - r^2} \] 3. Taking the limit as \( r \to 0 \): \[ \lim_{r \to 0} \frac{-1}{1 - r^2} = -1 \] Checking specific paths (e.g., along the x-axis, y-axis, or y = x) confirms the limit is consistently \(-1\). Using substitution \( t = x^2 + y^2 \) also confirms the limit as \( \lim_{t \to 0} \frac{\ln(1 - t)}{t} = -1 \). Thus, the final answer is: \[ \boxed{-1} \]

We can use the approximation ln(1 - u) ≈ -u - u²/2 - u³/3 - ..., so ln(1 - u)/u ≈ -1 - u/2 - u²/3 - ... which approaches -1 as u approaches 0. So even with substitution, same conclusion. Therefore, after checking multiple methods: polar coordinates, substitution, L'Hospital's Rule, and checking specific paths, all confirm that the limit is -1. Therefore, the answer should be -1. **Final Answer** \boxed{-1} </think> To evaluate the limit \( \displaystyle \lim_{(x,y)\to (0,0)}\frac{\ln(1-x^2-y^2)}{x^2+y^2} \), we can convert to polar coordinates. Let \( x = r \cos \theta \) and \( y = r \sin \theta \). Then, as \( (x, y) \to (0, 0) \), \( r \to 0 \). The limit becomes: \[ \lim_{r \to 0} \frac{\ln(1 - r^2)}{r^2} \] Using the Taylor series expansion for \( \ln(1 - z) \) around \( z = 0 \), we have: \[ \ln(1 - r^2) = -r^2 - \frac{r^4}{2} - \frac{r^6}{3} - \cdots \] Dividing by \( r^2 \): \[ \frac{\ln(1 - r^2)}{r^2} = -1 - \frac{r^2}{2} - \frac{r^4}{3} - \cdots \] As \( r \to 0 \), all higher-order terms vanish, leaving the limit as \( -1 \). Alternatively, applying L'Hospital's Rule to the limit \( \lim_{r \to 0} \frac{\ln(1 - r^2)}{r^2} \): 1. Differentiate the numerator and denominator with respect to \( r \): - Numerator: \( \frac{d}{dr} \ln(1 - r^2) = \frac{-2r}{1 - r^2} \) - Denominator: \( \frac{d}{dr} r^2 = 2r \) 2. Applying L'Hospital's Rule gives: \[ \frac{-2r / (1 - r^2)}{2r} = \frac{-1}{1 - r^2} \] 3. Taking the limit as \( r \to 0 \): \[ \lim_{r \to 0} \frac{-1}{1 - r^2} = -1 \] Checking specific paths (e.g., along the x-axis, y-axis, or y = x) confirms the limit is consistently \(-1\). Using substitution \( t = x^2 + y^2 \) also confirms the limit as \( \lim_{t \to 0} \frac{\ln(1 - t)}{t} = -1 \). Thus, the final answer is: \[ \boxed{-1} \]

Problem: Evaluate the limit \( \displaystyle \lim_{(x,y)\to (0,0)}\frac{\ln(1-x^2-y^2)}{x^2+y^2} \). Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Reference Answer: Okay, so I need to find the limit as (x, y) approaches (0, 0) of the function ln(1 - x² - y²) divided by (x² + y²). Hmm, let's see. I remember that limits involving multiple variables can sometimes be tricky because you have to consider all possible paths approaching the point, not just along straight lines. But maybe there's a way to simplify this expression first. First, I notice that the argument inside the natural logarithm is 1 - x² - y². As (x, y) approaches (0, 0), x² + y² approaches 0, so 1 - x² - y² approaches 1. And ln(1) is 0, so the numerator is approaching 0. The denominator x² + y² is also approaching 0. So we have a 0/0 indeterminate form. That makes me think that maybe L'Hospital's Rule could be applicable here. But wait, L'Hospital's Rule is for single-variable limits. How does that work in two variables? Hmm, perhaps if I convert to polar coordinates, since x² + y² is just r² in polar coordinates. Let me try that substitution. Let’s set x = r cosθ and y = r sinθ. Then as (x, y) approaches (0, 0), r approaches 0. So the limit becomes the limit as r approaches 0 of ln(1 - r²) divided by r². Since θ doesn't appear in the expression, the limit is the same regardless of the angle θ. That simplifies things because now it's a single-variable limit in terms of r. So now I have to compute lim_{r→0} ln(1 - r²)/r². This looks like a standard limit. Maybe I can use the Taylor series expansion for ln(1 - z) around z = 0. The Taylor series for ln(1 - z) is -z - z²/2 - z³/3 - ... for |z| < 1. So if I let z = r², then ln(1 - r²) = -r² - (r²)^2/2 - (r²)^3/3 - ... So then, ln(1 - r²)/r² would be (-r² - r^4/2 - r^6/3 - ...)/r² = -1 - r²/2 - r^4/3 - ... So as r approaches 0, all the terms with r in them go away, and the limit is -1. Therefore, the original limit is -1. Wait, let me check that again. If I expand ln(1 - r²) as -r² - r^4/2 - r^6/3 - ..., then dividing by r² gives -1 - r²/2 - r^4/3 - ... which clearly approaches -1 as r approaches 0. So that seems right. Alternatively, maybe I can use L'Hospital's Rule in the single-variable case after converting to polar coordinates. If we treat the limit as r approaches 0, the numerator is ln(1 - r²) and the denominator is r². Both numerator and denominator approach 0 as r approaches 0. So applying L'Hospital's Rule once, take the derivative of numerator and denominator with respect to r. The derivative of ln(1 - r²) with respect to r is (1/(1 - r²))*(-2r) = -2r/(1 - r²). The derivative of r² with respect to r is 2r. So the new expression after L'Hospital's Rule is (-2r/(1 - r²))/(2r) = (-2r)/(2r(1 - r²)) = -1/(1 - r²). Then take the limit as r approaches 0: -1/(1 - 0) = -1. So same result. Therefore, the limit is indeed -1. Another way to think about this is substitution. Let’s set t = x² + y², so when (x, y) approaches (0, 0), t approaches 0. Then the limit becomes lim_{t→0} ln(1 - t)/t. That's a single-variable limit, and we can apply L'Hospital's Rule here as well. The derivative of ln(1 - t) is -1/(1 - t), and the derivative of t is 1, so lim_{t→0} (-1/(1 - t))/1 = -1. So again, the same answer. Therefore, all these methods point to the limit being -1. I don't think there's any problem here because converting to polar coordinates simplifies the expression in a way that shows the limit is the same from all directions. So regardless of the path taken towards (0, 0), the value depends only on r approaching 0, hence the limit is -1. But just to be thorough, maybe I should check a few paths to make sure there's no funny business. Let's approach (0, 0) along the x-axis, where y = 0. Then the expression becomes ln(1 - x²)/x². Let’s compute the limit as x approaches 0. Substitute x = 0, we get ln(1)/0 = 0/0. Apply L'Hospital's Rule: derivative of numerator is (1/(1 - x²))*(-2x), derivative of denominator is 2x. So (-2x/(1 - x²))/(2x) = -1/(1 - x²). Then limit as x approaches 0 is -1/(1 - 0) = -1. Same result. What about approaching along y = x? Then x² + y² = 2x². So the expression becomes ln(1 - 2x²)/(2x²). Let’s take the limit as x approaches 0. Again, 0/0. Apply L'Hospital's Rule: derivative of numerator is (1/(1 - 2x²))*(-4x), denominator derivative is 4x. So (-4x/(1 - 2x²))/(4x) = -1/(1 - 2x²). As x approaches 0, the limit is -1. Same answer. So even along different paths, we get the same limit. Therefore, it's safe to conclude the limit is -1. Alternatively, maybe use the substitution u = x² + y². Then as (x, y) approaches (0, 0), u approaches 0. So the limit is lim_{u→0} ln(1 - u)/u. As before, this is a one-variable limit. We can use the approximation ln(1 - u) ≈ -u - u²/2 - u³/3 - ..., so ln(1 - u)/u ≈ -1 - u/2 - u²/3 - ... which approaches -1 as u approaches 0. So even with substitution, same conclusion. Therefore, after checking multiple methods: polar coordinates, substitution, L'Hospital's Rule, and checking specific paths, all confirm that the limit is -1. So then, ln(1 - r²)/r² would be (-r² - r^4/2 - r^6/3 - ...)/r² = -1 - r²/2 - r^4/3 - ... So as r approaches 0, all the terms with r in them go away, and the limit is -1. Therefore, the original limit is -1. Wait, let me check that again. If I expand ln(1 - r²) as -r² - r^4/2 - r^6/3 - ..., then dividing by r² gives -1 - r²/2 - r^4/3 - ... which clearly approaches -1 as r approaches 0. So that seems right. Alternatively, maybe I can use L'Hospital's Rule in the single-variable case after converting to polar coordinates. If we treat the limit as r approaches 0, the numerator is ln(1 - r²) and the denominator is r². Both numerator and denominator approach 0 as r approaches 0. So applying L'Hospital's Rule once, take the derivative of numerator and denominator with respect to r. The derivative of ln(1 - r²) with respect to r is (1/(1 - r²))*(-2r) = -2r/(1 - r²). The derivative of r² with respect to r is 2r. So the new expression after L'Hospital's Rule is (-2r/(1 - r²))/(2r) = (-2r)/(2r(1 - r²)) = -1/(1 - r²). Then take the limit as r approaches 0: -1/(1 - 0) = -1. So same result. Therefore, the limit is indeed -1. Another way to think about this is substitution. Let’s set t = x² + y², so when (x, y) approaches (0, 0), t approaches 0. Then the limit becomes lim_{t→0} ln(1 - t)/t. That's a single-variable limit, and we can apply L'Hospital's Rule here as well. The derivative of ln(1 - t) is -1/(1 - t), and the derivative of t is 1, so lim_{t→0} (-1/(1 - t))/1 = -1. So again, the same answer. Therefore, all these methods point to the limit being -1. I don't think there's any problem here because converting to polar coordinates simplifies the expression in a way that shows the limit is the same from all directions. So regardless of the path taken towards (0, 0), the value depends only on r approaching 0, hence the limit is -1. But just to be thorough, maybe I should check a few paths to make sure there's no funny business. Let's approach (0, 0) along the x-axis, where y = 0. Then the expression becomes ln(1 - x²)/x². Let’s compute the limit as x approaches 0. Substitute x = 0, we get ln(1)/0 = 0/0. Apply L'Hospital's Rule: derivative of numerator is (1/(1 - x²))*(-2x), derivative of denominator is 2x. So (-2x/(1 - x²))/(2x) = -1/(1 - x²). Then limit as x approaches 0 is -1/(1 - 0) = -1. Same result. What about approaching along y = x? Then x² + y² = 2x². So the expression becomes ln(1 - 2x²)/(2x²). Let’s take the limit as x approaches 0. Again, 0/0. Apply L'Hospital's Rule: derivative of numerator is (1/(1 - 2x²))*(-4x), denominator derivative is 4x. So (-4x/(1 - 2x²))/(4x) = -1/(1 - 2x²). As x approaches 0, the limit is -1. Same answer. So even along different paths, we get the same limit. Therefore, it's safe to conclude the limit is -1. Alternatively, maybe use the substitution u = x² + y². Then as (x, y) approaches (0, 0), u approaches 0. So the limit is lim_{u→0} ln(1 - u)/u. As before, this is a one-variable limit. We can use the approximation ln(1 - u) ≈ -u - u²/2 - u³/3 - ..., so ln(1 - u)/u ≈ -1 - u/2 - u²/3 - ... which approaches -1 as u approaches 0. So even with substitution, same conclusion. Therefore, after checking multiple methods: polar coordinates, substitution, L'Hospital's Rule, and checking specific paths, all confirm that the limit is -1. Therefore, the answer should be -1. **Final Answer** \boxed{-1} </think> To evaluate the limit \( \displaystyle \lim_{(x,y)\to (0,0)}\frac{\ln(1-x^2-y^2)}{x^2+y^2} \), we can convert to polar coordinates. Let \( x = r \cos \theta \) and \( y = r \sin \theta \). Then, as \( (x, y) \to (0, 0) \), \( r \to 0 \). The limit becomes: \[ \lim_{r \to 0} \frac{\ln(1 - r^2)}{r^2} \] Using the Taylor series expansion for \( \ln(1 - z) \) around \( z = 0 \), we have: \[ \ln(1 - r^2) = -r^2 - \frac{r^4}{2} - \frac{r^6}{3} - \cdots \] Dividing by \( r^2 \): \[ \frac{\ln(1 - r^2)}{r^2} = -1 - \frac{r^2}{2} - \frac{r^4}{3} - \cdots \] As \( r \to 0 \), all higher-order terms vanish, leaving the limit as \( -1 \). Alternatively, applying L'Hospital's Rule to the limit \( \lim_{r \to 0} \frac{\ln(1 - r^2)}{r^2} \): 1. Differentiate the numerator and denominator with respect to \( r \): - Numerator: \( \frac{d}{dr} \ln(1 - r^2) = \frac{-2r}{1 - r^2} \) - Denominator: \( \frac{d}{dr} r^2 = 2r \) 2. Applying L'Hospital's Rule gives: \[ \frac{-2r / (1 - r^2)}{2r} = \frac{-1}{1 - r^2} \] 3. Taking the limit as \( r \to 0 \): \[ \lim_{r \to 0} \frac{-1}{1 - r^2} = -1 \] Checking specific paths (e.g., along the x-axis, y-axis, or y = x) confirms the limit is consistently \(-1\). Using substitution \( t = x^2 + y^2 \) also confirms the limit as \( \lim_{t \to 0} \frac{\ln(1 - t)}{t} = -1 \). Thus, the final answer is: \[ \boxed{-1} \]

The limit becomes: \[ \lim_{r \to 0} \frac{\ln(1 - r^2)}{r^2} \] Using the Taylor series expansion for \( \ln(1 - z) \) around \( z = 0 \), we have: \[ \ln(1 - r^2) = -r^2 - \frac{r^4}{2} - \frac{r^6}{3} - \cdots \] Dividing by \( r^2 \): \[ \frac{\ln(1 - r^2)}{r^2} = -1 - \frac{r^2}{2} - \frac{r^4}{3} - \cdots \] As \( r \to 0 \), all higher-order terms vanish, leaving the limit as \( -1 \). Alternatively, applying L'Hospital's Rule to the limit \( \lim_{r \to 0} \frac{\ln(1 - r^2)}{r^2} \): 1. Differentiate the numerator and denominator with respect to \( r \): - Numerator: \( \frac{d}{dr} \ln(1 - r^2) = \frac{-2r}{1 - r^2} \) - Denominator: \( \frac{d}{dr} r^2 = 2r \) 2. Applying L'Hospital's Rule gives: \[ \frac{-2r / (1 - r^2)}{2r} = \frac{-1}{1 - r^2} \] 3. Taking the limit as \( r \to 0 \): \[ \lim_{r \to 0} \frac{-1}{1 - r^2} = -1 \] Checking specific paths (e.g., along the x-axis, y-axis, or y = x) confirms the limit is consistently \(-1\). Using substitution \( t = x^2 + y^2 \) also confirms the limit as \( \lim_{t \to 0} \frac{\ln(1 - t)}{t} = -1 \). Thus, the final answer is: \[ \boxed{-1} \]
